Agent by purchase is the opponent (khasm) of the pre-emptor. He (Quduri) said: Whoever buys a house for someone else, he is the opponent of the pre-emptor because he is the contractor, and taking by pre-emption is among the rights of the contract, so it is directed against him. He said: Unless he hands it over to the principal, for then he has no possession or ownership left, so the opponent is the principal. And this is because the agent is like the seller to the principal, as is known, so his handing over to him is like the seller's handing over to the buyer, thus litigation is with him. However, along with that, he (the agent) stands in the place of the principal, so his presence in litigation before delivery is sufficient. Similarly, if the seller is the executor of a deceased person regarding what is permissible for him to sell, due to what we have mentioned. Translation: Quduri said: Whoever buys a house for another, he is the opponent of the pre-emptor because he is the contractor, and taking by pre-emption is among the rights of the contract, so this right is directed against him. Quduri said: Unless he hands over the house to the principal, because for him there remains no possession or ownership, so the opponent becomes the principal. And this is because the agent is like the seller to the principal, as is known, so the agent's handing over to the principal is like the seller's handing over to the buyer, thus litigation will be with the principal. But he (the agent) stands in the place of the principal, so his presence in litigation before delivery is sufficient. Similarly, if the seller is the executor of a deceased person regarding things where his sale is permissible, due to the reason we have mentioned. Clarification: The principle is that in a sale, the rights of the contract revert to the agent. Therefore, if someone buys a house as an agent for another and has not yet handed it over to the principal, the pre-emptor can litigate with the agent and take pre-emption, because taking by pre-emption is also a right of the contract of sale, and rights revert to the agent. Yes, if the agent has handed it over to the principal, then litigation will not be with the agent because he has neither possession nor ownership. Because the agent and principal are like the buyer and seller, so the ruling that applied there applies between agent and principal. Question: When the agent is like the seller, in the case of the seller, the presence of the buyer was necessary. Is the presence of the principal necessary with the agent? And if not, how are the agent and principal like the seller? Answer: The seller was not the agent of the buyer, but the agent is the agent of his principal. When the agent is present, what need is there for the principal to be present? Therefore, a distinction arises. If the buyer is an agent, the same ruling applies; and if the seller is an agent, the same ruling applies: the pre-emptor should demand pre-emption from the seller's agent as long as it is in his possession, otherwise from the buyer. Similarly, the executor has the same ruling: when the executor has sold and the sold property is still in his possession, the pre-emptor can litigate with the executor.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Quduri's Mukhtasar regarding who the pre-emptor's opponent (khasm) is when an agent is involved. The ruling states that if an agent buys a property for his principal, the agent himself is the opponent for the pre-emptor, because the agent is the actual contracting party, and the right of pre-emption is a right arising from the contract. However, if the agent has already handed over the property to the principal, then the principal becomes the opponent, since the agent no longer has possession or ownership. The author explains that the agent is analogous to a buyer from the principal, so delivering to the principal is like the seller delivering to the buyer. A question arises: In a normal sale, the buyer must be present, but here the agent represents the principal. The answer is that the seller is not the principal's agent, but the agent is; therefore, the principal's presence is not required when the agent is present. The same ruling applies whether the agent is on the buyer's side or the seller's side. Additionally, the executor (wasi) of a deceased seller is treated similarly: the pre-emptor may litigate with the executor if the property is still in his possession.

Key Terms
وکیل (wakil): An agent who is authorized by another (the principal) to perform a legal act, such as buying a property.
خصم (khasm): The opponent or adversary in litigation; the person against whom the pre-emptor must make his claim.
موکل (muwakkil): The principal who authorizes an agent to act on his behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If an agent buys a house for his principal but has not yet handed it over, who is the opponent (khasm) for the pre-emptor?
A) The principal
B) The agent
C) Both the agent and principal
D) The seller

Key Takeaway
The agent is the contracting party and therefore the opponent for pre-emption until he delivers the property to the principal.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
8 min
The Opponent in Pre-emption When Buyer is an Agent or Executor
Ruling on who the shafi' faces when buyer is an agent or executor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct party (agent vs. principal) against whom a pre-emptor must claim, based on whether delivery has occurred.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor claims against the agent-contractor until delivery; after delivery, the principal becomes the opponent.

Pre-emption (shuf'ah) gives a co-owner or neighbor the right to replace the buyer in a property sale. But who is the buyer when the transaction is made through an agent? The Hanafi school clarifies that the agent is the immediate contractor, and until the property is delivered to the principal, the pre-emptor must claim against the agent. After delivery, the principal inherits the rights and obligations. This lesson unpacks the ruling from Al-Quduri's matn and the reasoning behind it, including the analogy to seller-agent and executor cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
In a pre-emption scenario, there is the seller, the buyer, and the pre-emptor (shafi'). Here, the buyer is acting as an agent (wakil) for a principal (muwakkil). The question is: whom should the shafi' sue?
The agent is the contractor, but he acts on behalf of someone else.
2
Apply the general rule
The matn states that the agent is the opponent because he is the actual contractor (al-aqid). The right of pre-emption is a right of the contract, so it is directed against the contractor.
This is the default position.
3
Consider the exception
If the agent has already delivered the property to the principal, the agent no longer has possession or ownership. Therefore, the principal becomes the opponent.
Delivery transfers the rights and obligations.
4
Understand the analogy
The agent is like a seller from the principal. Just as a seller hands over to a buyer, the agent's delivery to the principal shifts the dispute to the principal. However, the agent remains the representative, so his presence in court before delivery is sufficient.
This answers the potential objection about the principal's absence.
5
Extend to seller-agent and executor
The same rule applies when the seller is an agent for an absent person or an executor (wasi) of a deceased. The shafi' can claim against the agent-seller as long as the property is in his possession.
The principle is consistent: the contractor is the opponent.

Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on who the shafi' (pre-emptor) should claim against when the property has been purchased by an agent or executor. The matn states that the agent-buyer is the opponent (khasm) because he is the actual contractor, and the right of pre-emption is a right arising from the contract. However, if the agent has already delivered the property to the principal (muwakkil), then the principal becomes the opponent, as the agent no longer has possession or ownership. The commentary elaborates that the agent is like a seller from the principal, so delivery to the principal is like delivery from the seller to the buyer. Before delivery, the agent can be sued alone; after delivery, the principal must be sued. The same rules apply when the seller is an agent for an absent person or an executor. The commentary also answers a possible objection: the agent is a deputy of the principal, so his presence suffices, unlike the original seller who is not a deputy.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property sold to a third party by matching the price.
وکیل (wakil): Agent: one appointed to act on behalf of another in a legal transaction.
خصم (khasm): Opponent or adversary in a legal claim; the party against whom the shafi' must assert his right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the pre-emptor's opponent is the agent-contractor until delivery; after delivery, the principal becomes the opponent.

The Shafi' Has Rights of Inspection and Defect Options After Judgment
Ruling that the shafi' retains khiyar options after judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that the shafi' retains khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-'ayb after judgment, and explain the qiyas analogy with purchase that grounds this ruling.

Core Idea
Pre-emption is a new sale: the shafi' inherits all buyer's options, independent of the original buyer's actions.

This passage establishes an important principle: after a court awards pre-emption, the shafi' (pre-emptor) stands in the position of a buyer from the original purchaser. Therefore, all options available to a buyer in a sale, such as the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) and the option of defect (khiyar al-'ayb), are available to the shafi' even if the original buyer had waived them or already inspected the property.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is pre-emption (shuf'ah) after judgment. The ruling is that the shafi' has the option of inspection if he had not seen the property, and the option of defect if he finds a defect, regardless of the buyer's stipulation or inspection.
Focus on what the shafi' can do after judgment.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is qiyas (analogy) with purchase. The text states 'li-anna al-akhdh bi al-shuf'ah bi-manzilat al-shira' — taking by pre-emption is like purchase.
This is a qiyas jali because the common illa is exchange of property for property.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The wajh al-dalalah is the phrase 'bi-manzilat al-shira'' which indicates that the legal effects of purchase apply to pre-emption. Since in purchase the buyer has khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-'ayb, the same options apply to the shafi'.
The shafi' is effectively a buyer from the original buyer, so the buyer's options transfer.
4
Identify the ikhtilaf
This ruling is mukhtalaf fih between madhahib. The Hanafi school grants these options, but some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may not extend them to the shafi' in the same way.
The text does not detail the opposing view, but the Hanafi position is clear.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi based on the strong qiyas. The text affirms that the buyer's waiver or inspection does not bind the shafi' because the buyer is not the shafi's agent.
This reinforces the independence of the shafi's rights.

He said: And when the shafi' is granted judgment for the house and he had not seen it, then he has the option of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah). And if he finds a defect in it, he has the right to return it, even if the buyer had stipulated immunity from it. Because taking by pre-emption is like purchase; do you not see that it is an exchange of property for property? So both options are established in it, as in purchase. And it is not dropped by the buyer's stipulation of immunity, nor by his seeing it, because he is not an agent of the shafi', so he does not have the power to drop it. Translation: And when the shafi' is granted judgment for the house, even though he had not seen it, then the shafi' has the option of inspection. And if he finds a defect in that house, he has the right to return it, even if the buyer had stipulated immunity from defects. Because taking by pre-emption is like purchase; do you not see that it is an exchange of property for property? So both options are established in it, as in purchase. And the option is not dropped by the buyer's stipulation of immunity, nor by his seeing it, because the buyer is not an agent of the shafi', so he cannot drop the shafi's option. Explanation: Just as in a sale there is an option of inspection and an option of defect, so too in pre-emption, because the shafi' is effectively buying it from the buyer, so the rulings of sale apply here. If in a sale the seller stipulates immunity from defects, the option of defect is still not dropped. Similarly here. Question: The option of inspection should not be given when the buyer had purchased after seeing it? The buyer's seeing is not the shafi's seeing, because the buyer is not the shafi's agent or representative. Therefore, the buyer cannot nullify the shafi's option.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage establishes that after obtaining a judgment in favor of the shafi', he retains the right of inspection (khiyar al-ru'yah) and the right to return for defects (khiyar al-'ayb), even if the original buyer had waived them. The reasoning is that pre-emption is treated like a new sale between the shafi' and the buyer, so all buyer's options apply. The buyer's waiver or inspection does not affect the shafi' because the buyer is not the shafi's agent. The commentary addresses a potential objection: even if the buyer had seen the property, that does not constitute the shafi's inspection.

Key Terms
خیار الرؤیة (khiyar al-ru'yah): Option of inspection: the right of a buyer to annul a sale after seeing the item if it does not meet expectations.
خیار العیب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option of defect: the right to return an item if a defect is discovered.
شفعة (shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a property that is sold to an outsider, by paying the price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the shafi' retain the option of inspection even if the buyer had seen the property?
A) Because the shafi' is the real buyer
B) Because the buyer's seeing does not count as the shafi's seeing
C) Because the seller waived the option
D) Because pre-emption is not like purchase

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the shafi' retains khiyar al-ru'yah and khiyar al-'ayb after judgment, independent of the buyer's actions, because pre-emption is analogous to a new sale.

Resolving Price Disputes in Shuf'ah: The Purchaser's Word is Taken
Ruling on whose word is accepted when price disputed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for price disputes in shuf'ah by identifying the claimant and denier, and stating that the denier's word is accepted with his oath.

Core Idea
In shuf'ah price disputes, the purchaser's word is taken because he is the denier, not the claimant.

This passage from Imam al-Quduri's Mukhtasar establishes a key procedural rule for pre-emption cases. When the shafi' (pre-emptor) and the purchaser disagree on the sale price, the judge must accept the purchaser's statement provided he swears an oath. The ruling hinges on the Hanafi classification of who is the claimant (mudda'i) and who is the denier (munkir). Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the maxim: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties in dispute
The dispute is between the shafi' (pre-emptor) and the purchaser (mushtari) regarding the actual sale price of the property.
The shafi' claims a lower price to reduce his payment; the purchaser claims a higher price to receive more.
2
Determine the claimant (mudda'i)
Apply the Hanafi definition: the mudda'i is the one who, if he abandons his claim, leaves the other party free without obligation. The shafi' claims a right to take the house at a lower price, imposing an obligation on the purchaser. Thus, the shafi' is the claimant.
The purchaser does not demand anything from the shafi'; he simply denies the lower price.
3
Determine the denier (munkir)
The purchaser denies the shafi's claim that the price is lower. Therefore, he is the munkir.
The munkir's statement is preferred based on the hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath on the denier.'
4
Apply the procedural principle
Since the shafi' is the claimant, he must bring evidence. If he has no proof, the purchaser's word (with his oath) is accepted. The judge rules based on the purchaser's stated price.
The purchaser must swear an oath that the price is as he claims.
5
Explain why no mutual oath (tahaluf)
Mutual oaths (tahaluf) occur only when both parties are simultaneously claimants and deniers, as in a seller-purchaser price dispute (where each claims a different price and each denies the other's). Here, only the purchaser denies; the shafi' is a pure claimant, not a denier. Hence no tahaluf.
This distinguishes shuf'ah disputes from ordinary sale disputes.

Chapter on Dispute. Translation: A chapter on the statement of dispute. When the shafi' and the purchaser differ regarding the price, whose word is credited? He (Imam al-Quduri) said: And if the shafi' and the purchaser differ regarding the price, then the word is the word of the purchaser, because the shafi' claims entitlement to the house upon payment of the lesser amount, and the purchaser denies. And the word of the denier is accepted with his oath. And they do not take mutual oaths (tahaluf), because the shafi' claims entitlement from him, while the purchaser does not claim anything from the shafi', due to his (the shafi's) choice between abandoning and taking. And there is no text here, so they do not take mutual oaths. Translation: Imam al-Quduri said: And if the shafi' and the purchaser differ regarding the price, then the purchaser's word is accepted. Because the shafi' claims entitlement to the house upon payment of the lesser amount, and the purchaser is the denier. And the word of the denier is accepted with his oath. And they will not take mutual oaths, because the shafi' claims entitlement from him; the purchaser does not claim anything from the shafi', due to the shafi's choice between leaving and taking. And there is no text (nass) here, so they do not take mutual oaths. Explanation: In this chapter, those issues are mentioned that arise when there is a dispute between the shafi' and the purchaser, or between the shafi' and the seller, or between the seller and the purchaser. Example: Dispute between the shafi' and the purchaser regarding the amount of the price. The purchaser says: I bought this house for two thousand. The shafi' says: You bought it for one thousand. Whose word will be accepted? It is stated that the purchaser's word will be accepted. Because the established principle is that the claimant's duty is to produce witnesses, and the defendant's duty is to take an oath if the claimant fails. So here the shafi' is the claimant and the purchaser is the defendant. Therefore, either the shafi' brings witnesses, or if not, along with the oath, the purchaser's word is accepted. Question: Why is the shafi' the claimant? The claimant is one who imposes something on the other party by his claim. When we consider, this characteristic is found in the shafi' because he is imposing on the purchaser that he must give him the house at a lesser price, and that this is his right. But the purchaser is not imposing anything on the shafi', because the shafi' has the choice to take or leave. Therefore it is said that the shafi' is the claimant and the purchaser is the defendant and denier. Question: When neither of them has witnesses, just as in the case of seller and purchaser, an oath is taken from both, similarly should an oath be taken from both the purchaser and the shafi'? Answer: Taking an oath from both can only be when both deny. In the case of seller and purchaser, there is denial from both sides. Here, only the purchaser denies and the shafi' claims, not denies. So how can we analogize it to the case of seller and purchaser, when the text (only)...
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Imam al-Quduri's matn regarding disputes in pre-emption. It establishes the general principle for resolving price disputes between the shafi' (pre-emptor) and the purchaser (mushtari). The ruling: the purchaser's word is credited (al-qawl qawl al-mushtari). The reasoning is based on the Hanafi usul principle of mudda'i (claimant) and mudda'a 'alayh (defendant). The shafi' is the claimant because he asserts a right to take the property at a lower price, thereby imposing an obligation on the purchaser. The purchaser is the defendant because he denies this. Since the defendant's word is taken with his oath, the purchaser does not need to produce witnesses; he swears that the price is as he claims. There is no mutual oath (tahaluf) because the purchaser is not claiming anything against the shafi'; only the shafi' has a claim. The commentary elaborates with an example: if the shafi' claims the price was 1000 and the purchaser claims it was 2000, the purchaser's statement is believed. It also answers potential questions: (1) Why is the shafi' the claimant? Because he seeks to enforce a right; the purchaser is neutral. (2) Why no mutual oath? Because mutual oaths require both parties to be deniers (mutakhasimun), as in the case of seller and purchaser where both claim a right. Here, only the purchaser denies; the shafi' is not denying anything, so there is no basis for tahaluf.

Key Terms
مدعی (mudda'i): The claimant: the one who asserts a right against another, and if he abandons his claim, he is free.
منکر (munkir): The denier: the one who denies the claim and whose word is accepted with oath.
تحالف (tahaluf): Mutual oath-taking by both parties in a dispute when each denies the other's claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a shuf'ah price dispute between shafi' and purchaser, why does the purchaser's word take precedence over the shafi's?
A) Because the shafi' is considered a liar.
B) Because the shafi' is the claimant and the purchaser is the defendant (denier).
C) Because the purchaser paid the full price.
D) Because the judge favors the purchaser.

Key Takeaway
In a shuf'ah price dispute, the purchaser's word is accepted with his oath because he is the denier, and mutual oaths do not apply as only one party denies.

Buyer's Word Accepted in Pre-emption Price Dispute
Ruling on whose word is accepted when pre-emptor and buyer dispute the price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when the pre-emptor and buyer dispute the price and identify who bears the burden of proof.

Core Idea
The buyer's word is accepted because he is the denier; the pre-emptor must prove his claim.

In cases of pre-emption (shuf'a), disputes often arise about the actual price paid for the property. The Hanafi school has a clear rule: the buyer's statement is taken as correct when no evidence exists. This lesson explains the reasoning behind this rule, focusing on the roles of claimant and denier in Islamic legal procedure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the dispute
The pre-emptor (shafi') claims the property should be conveyed to him at a lower price, while the buyer (mushtari) states the price was higher.
The dispute is about the amount of the price (thaman).
2
Determine who is the claimant (mudda'i) and who is the denier (munkir)
The pre-emptor is the claimant because he seeks to compel the buyer to hand over the property at a reduced price. The buyer is the denier because he denies the pre-emptor's claim.
A claimant is one who imposes a duty on another; the denier merely denies.
3
Apply the principle: qawl al-munkir
The general rule is that the denier's word is accepted with an oath, and the claimant must produce evidence. Since the buyer is the denier, his word is taken with his oath.
This principle is derived from the hadith: 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies.'
4
State the ruling
Therefore, the buyer's word is accepted as to the price, provided he takes an oath. The pre-emptor must either bring witnesses or accept the buyer's statement.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

شفیع اور مشتری کا ثمن میں اختلاف ہو جائے تو کس کا قول معتبر ہوگا
قال وان اختلف الشفيع والمشترى فى الثمن فالقول قول المشترى لأن الشفيع يدعى استحقاق الدار عليه عند نقد الاقل وهو ينكر والقول قول المنكر مع يمينه ولا يتحالفان لأن الشفيع ان كان يدعى عليه استحقاق الدار فالمشترى لا يدعى عليه شيئا لتخيره بين الترك والاخذ ولا نص ههنا فلا يتحالفان
ترجمہ امام قدوری نے فرمایا اور اگر شفیع و مشتری ثمن میں اختلاف کریں تو مشتری کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ اس لئے کہ شفیع اس پر دعوی کر ہے گھر کے استحقاق کا اقل کی ادائیگی کی صورت میں اور مشتری منکر ہے اور قول منکر کا معتبر ہوتا ہے مع اس کی یمین کے اور دونوں قسم نہیں کھائیں گے۔ اسلئے کہ شفیع اگر چہ اس کے اوپر استحقاق دار کا دعویٰ کرتا ہے۔ پس مشتری تو شفیع پر کسی چیز کا دعوی نہیں کرتا۔ شفیع کے مختار ہونے کی وجہ سے چھوڑنے اور لینے کے درمیان اور یہاں کوئی نص نہیں تو دونوں قسم نہیں کھائیں گے۔ تشریح اس فصل کے اندر وہ مسائل بیان کئے جائیں گے جو اس وقت سامنے آتے ہیں جبکہ مشتری اور شفیع یا شفیع اور بائع یا بائع اور مشتری کے درمیان اختلاف ہو جائے۔ مثال مشتری اور شفیع کے درمیان مقدار ثمن کے بارے میں اختلاف ہو جائے ۔ مشتری یہ کہے کہ میں نے یہ مکان دو ہزار میں خریدا ہے اور شفیع کہے کہ تو نے ایک ہزار میں خریدا ہے تو کس کا قول معتبر ہوگا تو بتایا کہ مشتری کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔ اسلئے کہ اصول یہ مقرر ہے کہ مدعی کا فریضہ ہے گواہ قائم کرنا، اور مدعی علیہ کا فریضہ ہے مدعی کے عاجز ہونے کی صورت میں قسم کھا لینا۔ تو یہاں مدعی شفیع اور مشتری مدعی علیہ ہے۔ لہذا یا تو شفیع گواہ لائے ورنہ مع الیمین مشتری کا قول معتبر ہوگا۔
سوال مدعی شفیع کیوں ہے؟ مدعی اس کو کہتے ہیں جو اپنے دعوئی سے دوسرے فریق پر کچھ لازم کر دے اور جب ہم نے غور کیا تو یہ وصف شفیع میں ملا کہ وہ مشتری پر یہ لازم کر رہا ہے کہ مجھے کم نشن میں یہ مکان دے اور یہ میرا حق ہے۔ لیکن مشتری شفیع پر کچھ لازم نہیں کر رہا ہے کیونکہ شفیع کو تو اختیار ہے چاہے شفعہ لے چاہے چھوڑ دے۔ اسلئے کہا گیا کہ مدعی شفیع ہے اور مشتری مدعی علیہ اور منکر ہے۔ سوال جب ان میں سے کسی کے پاس گواہ نہیں تو جیسے بائع اور مشتری سے ایسے موقعہ پر حلف لیا جاتا ہے ایسے ہی مشتری اور شفیع ہے بھی حلف لیا جائے؟ جواب دونوں سے قسم لینا دونوں کے انکار کی صورت میں ہو سکتا ہے بائع اور مشتری والی صورت میں دونوں کا انکار موجود ہے اور یہاں صرف مشتری کا انکار اور شفیع کا دعوی ہے نہ کہ انکار، تو پھر اس کو بائع اور مشتری کے مسئلہ پر کیسے قیاس کر سکتے ہیں ۔ جبکہ نص صرف
بائع اور مشتری کے سلسلہ میں وارد ہوئی ہے اور یہ مور د شرع کے درجہ میں ہے نہیں اسلئے ہم نے تخالف کو اختیار نہیں کیا بلکہ مشتری کے قول کو
معتبر مان لیا ہے۔
Section on dispute. Translation: The chapter is about dispute. Statement: And if the pre-emptor and buyer dispute about the price, then the say is the say of the buyer, because the pre-emptor claims entitlement of the house upon him at the time of paying the lesser [amount], and he denies, and the say of the denier is accepted with his oath, and they do not take mutual oaths, because the pre-emptor if he claims entitlement of the house upon him, the buyer does not claim anything upon him due to his choice between abandonment and taking, and there is no text here, so they do not take mutual oaths. Translation: Imam Quduri said: 'And if the pre-emptor and buyer dispute regarding the price, then the saying is the saying of the buyer.' This is because the pre-emptor claims entitlement of the house from him in the case of paying the lesser, and the buyer is the denier, and the saying of the denier is accepted along with his oath. And they will not take oaths because the pre-emptor, although he claims entitlement of the house upon him, the buyer does not claim anything against the pre-emptor because of the pre-emptor's choice between leaving and taking. And there is no text here, so they will not take oaths. Explanation: In this chapter, those issues will be explained that arise when there is a dispute between buyer and pre-emptor, or pre-emptor and seller, or seller and buyer. Example: Dispute between buyer and pre-emptor concerning the amount of price. The buyer says 'I bought this house for two thousand' and the pre-emptor says 'You bought it for one thousand'. Then whose saying will be accepted? It is stated that the saying of the buyer will be accepted. This is because the established principle is that the burden of the claimant is to produce witnesses, and the burden of the defendant is to take an oath if the claimant fails. So here the claimant is the pre-emptor and the defendant is the buyer. Therefore, either the pre-emptor brings witnesses or, with an oath, the buyer's saying is accepted. Question: Why is the pre-emptor the claimant? A claimant is one who, by his claim, imposes something on the other party. When we consider, we find this attribute in the pre-emptor: he imposes on the buyer that he must give him this house for the lesser price, and that this is his right. But the buyer is not imposing anything on the pre-emptor because the pre-emptor has the option: he may take the pre-emption or leave it. Therefore, it is said that the claimant is the pre-emptor and the buyer is the defendant and denier. Question: When neither of them has witnesses, just as an oath is taken from seller and buyer on such occasions, so should an oath be taken from buyer and pre-emptor? Answer: Taking oaths from both is possible only when both deny. In the case of seller and buyer, both denials exist. Here, only the buyer denies and the pre-emptor claims, not denies. So how can we analogize this to the issue of seller and buyer, when the text is only [for seller and buyer]? The text is only reported concerning the seller and buyer, and this case is not included in the domain of the text. Therefore, we did not adopt mutual oaths but accepted the saying of the buyer.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Imam al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, which is a foundational text in the Hanafi school. The ruling addresses a common scenario in shuf'a (pre-emption) law: when the pre-emptor (shafi') and the buyer (mushtari) disagree on the price paid for the property, and neither party has any evidence, the buyer's word is taken as correct. The reasoning is grounded in the general principle of evidence law: the claimant (mudda'i) must prove his claim, while the denier (munkir) may simply take an oath. Here, the pre-emptor is the claimant because he seeks to compel the buyer to hand over the property at a lower price. The buyer, who denies the lower price, is the denier. Hence, the buyer's statement is accepted with his oath. The passage also explains why mutual oaths (tahaluf) are not taken in this case, unlike in a dispute between seller and buyer. The difference is that in a seller-buyer dispute, both parties are claimants and deniers in a sense (each denies the other's claim), so mutual oaths are prescribed. Here, only the buyer denies; the pre-emptor does not deny anything but rather asserts a right. Thus, the analogy to seller-buyer fails, and the Hanafi school relies on istihsan (juristic preference) to apply the standard rule of qawl al-munkir. The commentary in Urdu elaborates on these points, confirming that the buyer's word is taken unless the pre-emptor can produce evidence.

Key Terms
شفیع (shafiʿ): The pre-emptor: one who has the right of pre-emption in a property sale.
مشتری (mushtari): The buyer: the person who purchased the property and is being asked to convey it to the pre-emptor.
ثمن (thaman): The price paid or agreed upon in a sale.
مدعی (muddaʿi): The claimant: the party who asserts a right and bears the burden of proof.
منکر (munkir): The denier: the party who denies the claim and whose word is accepted with an oath.
یمین (yamin): An oath taken by the denier to affirm his denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this lesson, when the pre-emptor and buyer dispute the price and neither has evidence, whose word is accepted?
A) The pre-emptor's
B) The buyer's
C) Mutual oaths are taken
D) The seller's

Key Takeaway
In a pre-emption price dispute, the buyer's word is accepted because he is the denier and the pre-emptor is the claimant who must prove his claim.

10 min
Why Mutual Oaths Are Not Taken in Pre-emptor-Buyer Disputes
Explanation of why mutual oaths are not taken in pre-emptor-buyer disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects the analogy to seller-buyer disputes and applies istihsan in pre-emptor-buyer disputes.

Core Idea
Mutual oaths require both parties to be deniers; in pre-emption, only the buyer denies.

The previous rule established that the buyer's word is taken. But why aren't mutual oaths (tahaluf) applied as they are in seller-buyer disputes? This lesson delves into the legal reasoning: the distinction lies in who is denying what. Understanding this sharpens your ability to see how usul principles like qiyas and istihsan operate in fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recall the ruling for seller-buyer disputes
When seller and buyer disagree on the price, mutual oaths are taken because both are claimants and deniers simultaneously (the seller claims a higher price, the buyer a lower one).
This is established by a specific textual proof (nas).
2
Compare with pre-emptor-buyer dispute
In pre-emption, only the buyer denies the pre-emptor's claim; the pre-emptor does not deny anything but asserts a right. Therefore, only one party is a denier.
The pre-emptor is the claimant, not a denier.
3
Identify the attempted analogy (qiyas)
A student might analogize to seller-buyer disputes and argue for mutual oaths. The text rejects this because the underlying cause (illa) for mutual oaths—both parties denying—is absent.
The analogy is qiyas khafi (hidden analogy) because it seems similar but has a crucial difference.
4
Explain the application of istihsan
Instead of applying qiyas, the Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to apply the general rule of qawl al-munkir: the denier's word is accepted with an oath.
Istihsan here means preferring the clear principle over a weak analogy.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Imam al-Quduri's Mukhtasar, which is a foundational text in the Hanafi school. The ruling addresses a common scenario in shuf'a (pre-emption) law: when the pre-emptor (shafi') and the buyer (mushtari) disagree on the price paid for the property, and neither party has any evidence, the buyer's word is taken as correct. The reasoning is grounded in the general principle of evidence law: the claimant (mudda'i) must prove his claim, while the denier (munkir) may simply take an oath. Here, the pre-emptor is the claimant because he seeks to compel the buyer to hand over the property at a lower price. The buyer, who denies the lower price, is the denier. Hence, the buyer's statement is accepted with his oath. The passage also explains why mutual oaths (tahaluf) are not taken in this case, unlike in a dispute between seller and buyer. The difference is that in a seller-buyer dispute, both parties are claimants and deniers in a sense (each denies the other's claim), so mutual oaths are prescribed. Here, only the buyer denies; the pre-emptor does not deny anything but rather asserts a right. Thus, the analogy to seller-buyer fails, and the Hanafi school relies on istihsan (juristic preference) to apply the standard rule of qawl al-munkir. The commentary in Urdu elaborates on these points, confirming that the buyer's word is taken unless the pre-emptor can produce evidence.

مشتری (mushtari): The buyer: the person who purchased the property and is being asked to convey it to the pre-emptor.
ثمن (thaman): The price paid or agreed upon in a sale.
مدعی (muddaʿi): The claimant: the party who asserts a right and bears the burden of proof.
منکر (munkir): The denier: the party who denies the claim and whose word is accepted with an oath.
یمین (yamin): An oath taken by the denier to affirm his denial.
تحالف (tahāluf): Mutual oaths taken by both parties when they dispute and both deny each other's claims.

Why does the Hanafi school NOT apply mutual oaths (tahaluf) in a pre-emptor-buyer price dispute?

Key Takeaway
Mutual oaths are not taken in pre-emptor-buyer disputes because the pre-emptor is a claimant, not a denier; the ruling is derived by istihsan, rejecting a weak analogy to seller-buyer disputes.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Whose Evidence Prevails in Shuf'a?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on evidence preference in shuf'a

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf regarding evidence preference in shuf'a, and apply the principle of 'muthbit ziyadat' to analogous cases.

Core Idea
When both parties bring evidence, the pre-emptor's evidence is preferred according to the majority of Hanafi imams.

In shuf'a disputes, when both the pre-emptor and the buyer present witnesses regarding the price, whose evidence should the qadi accept? The matn of Quduri records a well-known intra-Hanafi disagreement. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each position, including the three analogies (qiyas) that Abu Yusuf uses, and the beginning of the counter-argument by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ikhtilaf
State the two positions: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad give preference to the pre-emptor's evidence; Abu Yusuf gives preference to the buyer's evidence.
The matn attributes the majority view to the two imams, indicating it is the relied-upon position.
2
Analyze Abu Yusuf's Dalil
Break down his three analogies (seller-buyer, principal-agent, slave from Dar al-Harb) and identify the common illa: the evidence that establishes an increase (higher price) is preferred.
Abu Yusuf's argument is based on the principle that the evidence that proves a greater fact is more worthy.
3
Examine the Response of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad
Even though the passage cuts off, note their distinction: the shuf'a case involves two potential sales, and rescission does not apply to the pre-emptor, unlike the seller-buyer case.
The full response is expected to be completed in the following lines, but the key point is the distinction based on the nature of shuf'a.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
If the pre-emptor's evidence is accepted, he takes the property at the lower price he claims; if the buyer's evidence is accepted, he pays the higher price.
The practical consequence directly affects the pre-emptor's financial obligation.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
The position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is the mufta bihi because it is from the majority (the two imams) and is supported by the matn's presentation.
This is consistent with the Hanafi reliance on the majority opinion among the three major imams.

طرفین کی دلیل
ولهما انه لاتنا في بينهما فيجعل كان الموجود بيعان وللشفيع ان ياخذ بايهما شاء وهذا بخلاف البائع مع المشترى لانه لا يتوالى بينهما عقد ان الابانفساخ الاول وههنا الفسخ لا يظهر في حق الشفيع
He said: And if both bring evidence, then the evidence is for the pre-emptor according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf said: the evidence is the evidence of the buyer because it is more establishing, so it becomes like the evidence of the seller, the agent, and the buyer from the enemy. Translation: Imam Quduri said: And if both of them (the buyer and the pre-emptor) establish evidence, then according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, the evidence of the pre-emptor will be considered valid, and Abu Yusuf said that the evidence of the buyer will be considered valid. This is because this (the buyer's evidence) is greater in terms of establishing, so it becomes like the evidence of the seller, the agent, and the buyer from the enemy. Explanation: In the first case, the buyer's word was accepted, but if both have established witnesses, then according to the two masters (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), the pre-emptor's witnesses are accepted, and according to Imam Abu Yusuf, the buyer's. Now Imam Abu Yusuf presents an argument and some evidences: The work of witnesses is to establish what is unestablished. In such a case, that is considered, and whichever has the greater aspect of establishment is given weight. This is found in the buyer's witnesses because they establish an increase. So they will be given weight. On this, he presents three evidences: 1. If a seller and buyer dispute about the amount of price and both establish witnesses, unanimously the seller's witnesses are accepted. This is because they establish an increase; similarly here the buyer's witnesses should be accepted. 2. If an agent and principal dispute about the amount of price, the agent's witnesses are accepted because they establish an increase; similarly here. 3. If infidels from Dar al-Harb capture a Muslim's slave from Dar al-Islam, and then a Muslim trader goes there with safe-conduct and buys that slave, then the Shariah gave an option to the original owner (the Muslim who previously owned him) and the one who bought and brought him from Dar al-Harb. The original owner, if he wishes, can buy that slave from the trader for the amount he bought him from Dar al-Harb. Now if they dispute about that price and both establish witnesses, then the witnesses of the trader (i.e., the one who bought from the enemies) will be accepted. Similarly here, the buyer's witnesses should be accepted. The evidence of the two (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad): And for them, that there is no delay between them, so it is made as if two sales exist, and the pre-emptor can take whichever he wishes. This differs from the case of seller and buyer because between them two contracts do not occur except by rescission of the first, and here the rescission does not appear in regard to the pre-emptor.

Commentary
This passage introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a more complex scenario: when both the pre-emptor and the buyer produce evidence (bayyina) to support their conflicting claims about the price. The matn from Quduri states the divergent positions. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the pre-emptor's evidence is preferred. According to Abu Yusuf, the buyer's evidence is preferred. The commentary then explains Abu Yusuf's reasoning in detail. He argues that the buyer's evidence is 'more establishing' (akthar ithbatan) because it proves an additional fact: a higher price. He supports this with three analogies: (1) In a dispute between seller and buyer about price, the seller's witnesses are accepted because they prove a higher price (the seller claiming more). (2) In a dispute between principal and agent about price, the agent's witnesses are accepted. (3) In the case of a slave captured from Dar al-Harb and repurchased by a trader, when the original owner and the trader dispute the price, the trader's witnesses are accepted. These analogies support giving preference to the party whose evidence asserts a higher price (i.e., the buyer). The passage then introduces the response of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (labeled 'Party of the two' - walahuma), but the text cuts off mid-sentence. Their argument begins: they state that there is no delay between the two sales (??) and that it is as if two sales exist, giving the pre-emptor the right to choose. They distinguish the present case from the seller-buyer dispute because in the shuf'a case, rescission does not appear in regard to the pre-emptor. The full reasoning is expected to be completed on the next page.

Key Terms
بینة (bayyinah): Evidence, usually two male witnesses, that establishes a fact in court.
شفیع (shafiʿ): The pre-emptor.
مشتری (mushtari): The buyer.
مثبت زیادت (muthbit ziyadat): One who establishes an increase (a higher price) through evidence.

Increase in Price by Buyer Does Not Bind the Pre-emptor
Explains that a voluntary price increase by buyer does not affect pre-emptor's right

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a buyer's voluntary price increase does not bind the pre-emptor and apply the qiyas reasoning to distinguish the two forms of increase.

Core Idea
A pre-emptor's right is fixed at the original price; any voluntary increase by the buyer is not binding.

This lesson examines a specific ruling in the law of pre-emption (shuf'ah). When a buyer voluntarily increases the price paid to the seller, does this affect the pre-emptor's right? The Hanafi school answers with a clear principle: the increase is not binding on the pre-emptor, who may take the property at the original price. We will explore the reasoning based on harm (darar) and analogy to contract renewal.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling and its basis
Identify that the buyer's increase does not bind the pre-emptor, and understand that the pre-emptor's right is attached to the original price.
The ruling is explicit: 'the increase is not binding'.
2
Understand the rationale: benefit vs. harm
Recognize that a decrease benefits the pre-emptor and is taken into account, while an increase harms him and is ignored.
Harm (darar) is the key illa for ignoring the increase.
3
Analyze the qiyas analogy
The text analogizes to renewal of contract at a higher price: the pre-emptor is also not bound. Identify the effective cause (illa) as the harm to the pre-emptor.
The analogy covers both forms of increase.
4
Distinguish the two forms of increase
Know that both scenarios (increase without renewal and renewal at higher price) yield the same ruling: the pre-emptor takes at the first price.
The text explicitly lists both forms.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that renewal binds the pre-emptor to the new price. Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf.

وان زاد المشترى للبائع لم تلزم الزيادة لان فيه منفعة له ونظير الزيادة اذا جدد العقد باكثر من الثمن الأول لم يلزم الشفيع حتى كان له ان ياخذها بالثمن الأول لما بينا كذا هذا
ترجمہ اور اگر مشتری نے بائع کیلئے ثمن میں زیادتی کر دی تو شفیع کے حق میں زیادتی لازم نہ ہوگی ۔ اسلئے کہ زیادتی کا اعتبار کرنے میں شفیع کا ضرر ہے۔ شفیع کے مستحق ہونے کی وجہ سے لینے کا اس سے ( زیادتی سے) کم کے ساتھ بخلاف کم کر دینے کے اس لئے کہ اس میں شفیع کی منفعت ہے اور زیادتی کی نظیر ہے۔ جبکہ مشتری نے عقد کی تجدید کی ہو ثمن اول سے اکثر کے ساتھ تو شفیع کو اکثر لازم نہ ہوں گے۔ یہاں تک کہ شفیع کو حق ہے کہ وہ گھر کو پہلے ثمن کے بدلہ میں لے لے۔ اسی دلیل کی وجہ سے جو ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں۔ ایسے ہی یہ ہے (یعنی بغیر تجدید کے اضافہ )۔
تشریح بائع کی کمی تو شفیع کیلئے نافع تھی اسلئے اس کا اعتبار کر لیا گیا لیکن اگر مشتری بائع کیلئے ثمن میں زیادتی کر دے تو یہ شفیع کیلئے مضر پڑے گی ۔ اسلئے اس کا اعتبار نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ شفیع پہلے ہی ثمن کے بدلہ اس کو لینے کا مستحق ہو چکا تھا۔
پھر اس زیادتی کی دو صورتیں ہیں۔
1- عقد اول برقرار ہے۔ اور اس میں ثمن کا اضافہ کر دیا جائے۔
2- عقدہی کی تجدید کی جائے اور ثمن اول سے زیادہ اس میں ثمن مقرر کیا جائے ۔۔

Then the noble author says: We have already explained the issue of decrease and increase in price in Kitab al-Buyu' (p. 59, vol. 3). If the buyer increases the price for the seller, this increase is not binding on the pre-emptor. And if the buyer increases for the seller, the increase does not become binding, because there is benefit for him in it. And the analogy of the increase is: if he renews the contract with more than the first price, the pre-emptor is not bound by the increase, so much so that he has the right to take it at the first price, as we have explained. Similarly, this is the case (i.e., increase without renewal). Translation: And if the buyer increases the price for the seller, the increase is not binding on the pre-emptor, because considering the increase causes harm to the pre-emptor. Since he is entitled to take it at less than that (the increase), unlike a decrease because that benefits the pre-emptor. And the analogy of the increase is when the buyer renews the contract with more than the first price; then the pre-emptor is not bound by the increase, so much so that the pre-emptor has the right to take the house for the first price. By the same proof we have mentioned, this is the same (i.e., increase without renewal). Explanation: The seller's decrease was beneficial for the pre-emptor, so it was taken into account. But if the buyer increases the price for the seller, it is harmful to the pre-emptor, so it will not be considered, because the pre-emptor had already become entitled to take it for the first price. Then there are two forms of this increase: 1) The first contract remains, and the price is increased in it. 2) The contract itself is renewed, and a price higher than the first is set. In the latter case, the increase is not binding on the pre-emptor, and similarly, the increase in the first form is also not binding on the pre-emptor.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah). The author states that if the buyer voluntarily increases the price agreed with the seller, this increase does not bind the pre-emptor. The reasoning is that the pre-emptor's right is established based on the original price; any subsequent increase would cause harm (darar) to him. The text analogizes this to a scenario where the buyer and seller renew the contract at a higher price — in that case, the pre-emptor is also not bound by the new price and may take the property at the original price. The commentary clarifies that a decrease in price benefits the pre-emptor and is thus taken into account, but an increase is detrimental and ignored. Two scenarios are enumerated: (1) the original contract remains valid but the price is increased, and (2) a new contract is made with a higher price. In both, the pre-emptor's right is protected at the first price.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a property that has been sold to a third party, by paying the price and taking the property from the buyer.
ثمن (thaman): The price paid for the property in the sale.
زیادت (ziyadah): An increase in the price voluntarily given by the buyer to the seller.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The pre-emptor must pay the increased price.
B) The pre-emptor may take the property at the original price.
C) The pre-emptor must pay the increased price if the contract is renewed.
D) The pre-emptor loses the right of pre-emption.

Key Takeaway
A buyer's voluntary price increase does not affect the pre-emptor's right; he always takes at the original sale price.

Pre-emption: Payment When Price is a Commodity or Property
Rulings on shuf'ah when the sale price is a commodity or property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for shuf'ah when the sale price is a commodity ('ard) or property, distinguishing between dhawat al-qiyam and dhawat al-amthal to determine the correct payment.

Core Idea
For pre-emption, pay value for unique items (dhwat al-qiyam) and like for fungibles (dhwat al-amthal).

In this lesson, we examine the rulings of shuf'ah when the buyer purchased the property not with cash but with a commodity or another property. The matn of al-Quduri provides clear guidance based on the category of the price: unique items require payment of value, while fungibles require payment of an equivalent. Understanding this distinction is essential for correct application of pre-emption rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of price
Determine whether the sale price was a commodity ('ard), a makil/mawzun item, or another property (real estate). This classification triggers different payment rules for the pre-emptor.
The matn explicitly addresses three scenarios: 'ard, makil/mawzun, and property-for-property.
2
Classify the price as dhawat al-qiyam or dhawat al-amthal
For a commodity or property, assess whether it belongs to dhawat al-qiyam (unique items valued individually) or dhawat al-amthal (fungibles with standard substitutes). Makil and mawzun items are always dhawat al-amthal; 'ard can be either.
Dhawat al-amthal include grains, oils, and counted items with negligible price variation (adadi mutaqarib).
3
Apply the rule: value for unique, like for fungibles
If the price is dhawat al-qiyam, the pre-emptor pays the market value (qimah) of that item. If dhawat al-amthal, the pre-emptor gives an equivalent (mithl) in quantity and quality.
The principle is to compensate the buyer with what he gave, to the extent possible.
4
Understand the qiyas to itlaf (destruction)
The ruling is analogized to tort law: if someone destroys property, compensation is by the like for fungibles and value for unique items. Here, the pre-emptor 'takes' the property and must give back the equivalent.
The effective cause ('illa) is the need to restore the buyer to his original position as closely as possible.
5
Note the inter-madhhab difference
The Hanafi school differentiates based on price category, while Imam al-Shafi'i (in one view) requires the pre-emptor to always pay the exact same item, regardless of category.
This difference leads to practical variation in cases like property exchange or unique commodities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن اشترى دارا بعرض اخذها الشفيع بقيمته لانه من ذوات القيم وان اشتراها بمكيل اوموزون اخذها بمثله لانهما من ذوات الامثال وهذا لان الشرع اثبت للشفيع ولاية التملك على المشترى بمثل ما تملكه فیراعى بالقدر الممكن كمافى الاتلاف والعددى المتقارب من ذوات الأمثال وان باع عقار ابعقار اخذ الشفيع كل واحد منهما بقيمة الاخر لانه بدله وهو من ذوات القيم فياخذه بقيمته
ترجمہ - امام قدوری نے فرمایا اور جس نے کوئی گھر خریدا کسی سامان کے بدلے تو شفیع اس گھر کو اس کی قیمت کے بدلہ لے گا۔ اسلئے کہ ذوات القیم میں سے ہے اور اگر گھر کو کسی مکیلی یا موزونی چیز کے بدلہ میں خریدا تو شفیع اس کو اس کے مثل کے بدلے میں لے گا۔ اسلئے کہ یہ دونوں ذوات الامثال میں سے ہیں اور یہ اسلئے کہ شریعت نے شفیع کیلئے مشتری پر تملک کی ولایت کو ثابت کیا ہے۔ اس کے مثل کے ساتھ کہ جس کے ساتھ مشتری مالک ہوا ہے تو اتلاف کے مثل بقدر ممکن اس کی رعایت کی جائے گی اور عددی متقارب ذوات الامثال میں سے ہے اور اگر زمین کو زمین کے بدلے میں فروخت کیا ہو تو لے لے گا۔ شفیع ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کو دوسری کی قیمت کے بدلہ۔ اسلئے کہ یہ (ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک ) اس کا ( دوسری کا ) بدلہ ہے اور وہ ذوات القیم میں سے ہے تو شفیع اس کو اس کی قیمت کے بدلہ لے لے گا۔
تشریح مشتری نے گھر کس چیز کے بدلے میں خریدا ہے اگر ثمن کے بدلہ میں ہے تو اس کی تفصیل تو گذر چلی ۔ اور اگر سامان کے بدلے میں ہے یعنی سونا اور چاندی کے علاوہ اور سامان کے بدلے تو اس سامان کی دو صورتیں ہیں۔
وہ سامان ذوات القیم میں سے ہے یا ذوات الامثال میں ہے۔ پہلی صورت میں شفیع اس سامان کی قیمت دے کر اس گھر کو لے گا اور دوسری صورت میں اس کا مثل دے گا۔ کیونکہ شریعت نے شفیع کو یہ حق دیا ہے کہ وہ مشتری کی ملکیت کا بغیر رضامندی کے مالک ہو جائے۔
لیکن اس نے جس چیز کے بدلہ میں ملکیت حاصل کی وہی چیز اس کو ملنی چاہئے تو جہاں تک ہو سکے مثل دینے کی پوری رعایت ہونی چاہئے۔ لہذا اگر مثل صوری دینے پر قدرت ہو تو مثل صوری دے اور نہ مثل معنوی۔ مثل صوری کا مطلب مثل ہے یعنی ذوات الامثال میں اور مثل معنوی کا مطلب قیمت ہے یعنی ذوات القیم میں اگر کوئی کسی کا سامان ضائع کر دے وہاں بھی یہی حکم ہے۔ پھر مصنف نے بتایا کہ عدی متقارب کو ذوات الامثال میں سے شمار کیا گیا ہے۔ اگر کسی نے اپنی زمین فروخت کی اور اس کے بدلے میں اپنی زمین دی اور ان دونوں زمینوں کے شفیع ہیں تو وہ کیا قیمت دے کر اس زمین کو لیں گے؟ تو بتایا کہ بائع کی زمین کا شفیع اس کے بدلہ میں مشتری کی زمین کی قیمت دے گا اور مشتری کی زمین کا شفیع بدلہ میں بائع کی زمین کی قیمت دے گا۔ اس لئے کہ زمین ذوات القیم میں سے ہے اسلئے اس کی قیمت دے کر ہر ایک شفیع اس کو لے لے گا۔
تنبیه عرض قیمتی سامان جوشی نہ ہو جیسے غلام وغیرہ۔ ذوات الامثال بغیر کسی تفاوت کے ان کا مثل بازار میں مل جائے اور اگر تفاوت ہو لیکن وہ عرفا قابل شمار نہ ہو تب بھی مشکلی کہلائے گا۔ ذوات القیم بازاروں میں جس کا مثل نہ پایا جائے۔
عددی متقارب جس کے افراد و آحاد کے درمیان قیمت میں تفاوت نہ ہو۔
He said: And whoever buys a house for a commodity, the pre-emptor takes it at its value, because it is of the category of things measured by value. And if he buys it for something measured by capacity or weight, the pre-emptor takes it at its like, because both are of the category of things measured by like. And this is because the Law has established for the pre-emptor the authority of ownership over the buyer with the like of what he has owned, so consideration is given to the extent possible, as in the case of destruction (itlaf), and the numerically similar (adadi mutaqarib) are among the things measured by like. And if he sells a property for a property, the pre-emptor of each one takes it at the value of the other, because it is its substitute (badal) and it is of the category of things measured by value, so he takes it at its value. Translation: Imam al-Quduri said: And whoever buys a house for a commodity, the pre-emptor takes the house at the value of that commodity, because it is from dhawat al-qiyam. And if he buys it for something measured by capacity or weight, the pre-emptor takes it at its like, because both are from dhawat al-amthal. And this is because the Law has established for the pre-emptor the authority of ownership over the buyer with the like of what he has owned, so consideration is given to the extent possible, as in the case of destruction (itlaf), and the numerically similar are among dhawat al-amthal. And if a property is sold for a property, the pre-emptor of each one takes it at the value of the other, because it is its substitute and it is from dhawat al-qiyam, so he takes it at its value. Explanation: The buyer bought the house in exchange for what? If it is in exchange for cash (thaman), its detail has already passed. And if it is in exchange for a commodity (i.e., other than gold and silver), then this commodity has two forms: it is either from dhawat al-qiyam or from dhawat al-amthal. In the first form, the pre-emptor takes the house by giving the value of that commodity; in the second form, he gives its like. Because the Law gave the pre-emptor the right to become owner of the buyer's property without his consent. But the thing in exchange for which he acquired ownership should be given to him. So, as far as possible, full consideration of giving the like should be made. Therefore, if he is able to give a formal like (mithl suri), he gives a formal like; otherwise, a conceptual like (mithl ma'nawi). Formal like means like in dhawat al-amthal, and conceptual like means value in dhawat al-qiyam. The same rule applies if someone destroys another's property. Then the author said that the numerically similar (adadi mutaqarib) is counted among dhawat al-amthal. If someone sold his land and in exchange gave his own land, and there are pre-emptors for both lands, then what price will they pay to take the land? He said: The pre-emptor of the seller's land gives in exchange the value of the buyer's land, and the pre-emptor of the buyer's land gives in exchange the value of the seller's land. Because land is from dhawat al-qiyam, so each pre-emptor takes it by giving its value. Note: 'Ard is valuable commodity, not money, such as slaves, etc. Dhawat al-amthal are those whose like is available in the market without any difference; if there is difference but it is not significant in custom, it is still called mithli. Dhawat al-qiyam are those whose like is not found in the markets. Numerically similar (adadi mutaqarib) are those among whose individuals there is no difference in price.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage provides rulings on how the pre-emptor pays for the property when the sale price was not cash but a commodity or another property. The matn from al-Quduri states: if the house was bought with a commodity ('ard), the pre-emptor takes it at the value (qimah) of that commodity if it is among dhawat al-qiyam (items valued individually, like slaves, animals, real estate); if it was bought with something measured by capacity or weight (makil or mawzun), the pre-emptor takes it by giving its like (mithl), since these are dhawat al-amthal (fungibles). The legal principle is that the pre-emptor acquires ownership with the same thing the buyer used to acquire ownership, as far as possible, analogous to the law of torts (itlaf). The explanation clarifies that if the commodity is from dhawat al-qiyam, value is paid; if from dhawat al-amthal, the like is given. For property-for-property exchanges, each pre-emptor pays the value of the other property. The tanbih (note) defines terms: 'ard is non-monetary valuable property; dhawat al-amthal are fungibles with standard equivalents; dhawat al-qiyam are unique items; adadi mutaqarib (numerically close) are considered among dhawat al-amthal if price differences among units are negligible.

Key Terms
عرض ('ard): A commodity other than gold, silver, and cash; includes goods like cloth, animals, etc.
ذوات القیم (dhawat al-qiyam): Items that are unique and do not have perfect substitutes; their value is determined by estimation.
ذوات الأمثال (dhawat al-amthal): Items that are fungible and have standard equivalents in the market; compensation is by giving the same quantity/quality.
عددی متقارب ('adadi mutaqarib): Items that are counted and whose individual units are roughly equal in value; treated as dhwat al-amthal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a house is bought in exchange for a slave (a dhawat al-qiyam item), how does the pre-emptor take the house?
A) He gives the same slave to the buyer.
B) He gives the value of the slave in cash.
C) He gives a different slave of similar value.
D) He takes the house for free because the price is not cash.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the pre-emptor's payment depends on the category of the price: value for dhawat al-qiyam, like for dhawat al-amthal.

Ruling on Preemption When the Price is Land or Goods
Explains how preemptor compensates when price is non-cash goods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on how a preemptor compensates a buyer when the price is land or goods, and distinguish between dhawaat al-qeem and dhawaat al-amthal.

Core Idea
The preemptor must give exactly what the buyer gave: value for unique goods, like for fungible goods.

In this passage, the author of Ashraf al-Hidayah explains the compensation rules for shuf'ah when the sale price is not cash but land or other goods. The key is classifying the goods as dhawaat al-qeem (unique) or dhawaat al-amthal (fungible). Understanding this distinction is essential for applying shuf'ah correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the price type
Determine whether the buyer paid land, a unique good, or a fungible good.
The passage deals with land-for-land and goods-for-property.
2
Classify the good
Decide if the good is dhawaat al-qeem (unique, like land, slaves) or dhawaat al-amthal (fungible, like grains, eggs).
'Adad mutaqarib' (numerically close items) is treated as fungible.
3
Apply the compensation rule
If dhawaat al-qeem, pay market value; if dhawaat al-amthal, give a like item.
This ensures the buyer gets exactly what he lost.
4
Consider the land-for-land scenario
Land is always dhawaat al-qeem, so each preemptor pays the value of the other land.
The passage gives this as an explicit example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة اگر زمین کو زمین کے بدلے میں فروخت کیا ہو تو لے لے گا۔ شفیع ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک کو دوسری کی قیمت کے بدلہ۔ اسلئے کہ یہ (ان دونوں میں سے ہر ایک ) اس کا ( دوسری کا ) بدلہ ہے اور وہ ذوات القیم میں سے ہے تو شفیع اس کو اس کی قیمت کے بدلہ لے لے گا۔ تشریح مشتری نے گھر کس چیز کے بدلے میں خریدا ہے اگر شمن کے بدلہ میں ہے تو اس کی تفصیل تو گذر چلی ۔ اور ا نر سامان لے بدل۔ میں ہے یعنی سونا اور چاندی کے علاوہ اور سامان کے بدلہ تو اس سامان کی دو صورتیں ہیں۔ وہ سامان ذوات القیم میں سے ہے یا ذوات الامثال میں ہے۔ پہلی صورت میں شفیع اس سامان کی قیمت دے کر اس گھر کو لے گا اور دوسری صورت میں اس کا مثل دے گا۔ کیونکہ شریعت نے شفیع کو یہ حق دیا ہے کہ وہ مشتری کی ملکیت کا بغیر رضامندی کے مالک ہو جائے۔ لیکن اس نے جس چیز کے بدلہ میں ملکیت حاصل کی وہی چیز اس کو ملنی چاہئے تو جہاں تک ہو سکے مثل دینے کی پوری رعایت ہونی چاہئے۔ لہذا اگر مثل صوری دینے پر قدرت ہو تو مثل صوری دے اور نہ مثل معنوی۔ مثل صوری کا مطلب مثل ہے یعنی ذوات الامثال میں اور مثل معنوی کا مطلب قیمت ہے یعنی ذوات القیم میں اگر کوئی کسی کا سامان ضائع کر دے وہاں بھی یہی حکم ہے۔ پھر مصنف نے بتایا کہ عدی متقارب کو ذوات الامثال میں سے شمار کیا گیا ہے۔ اگر کسی نے اپنی زمین فروخت کی اور اس کے بدلے میں اپنی زمین دی اور ان دونوں زمینوں کے شفیع ہیں تو وہ کیا قیمت دے کر اس زمین کو لیں گے؟ تو بتایا کہ بائع کی زمین کا شفیع اس کے بدلہ میں مشتری کی زمین کی قیمت دے گا اور مشتری کی زمین کا شفیع بدلہ میں بائع کی زمین کی قیمت دے گا۔ اس لئے کہ زمین ذوات القیم میں سے ہے اسلئے اس کی قیمت دے کر ہر ایک شفیع اس کو لے لے گا۔ تنبیه عرض قیمتی سامان جوشی نہ ہو جیسے غلام وغیرہ۔ ذوات الامثال بغیر کسی تفاوت کے ان کا مثل بازار میں مل جائے اور اگر تفاوت ہو لیکن وہ عرفا قابل شمار نہ ہو تب بھی مشکلی کہلائے گا۔ ذوات القیم بازاروں میں جس کا مثل نہ پایا جائے۔ عددی متقارب جس کے افراد و آحاد کے درمیان قیمت میں تفاوت نہ ہو۔
Chapter of Preemption. If land is sold in exchange for land, he (the preemptor) will take it. The preemptor (for each of the two lands) gives the value of the other (land) in exchange. Because this (each of the two) is the substitute for the other, and it is among goods of value (dhawaat al-qeem), so the preemptor takes it in exchange for its value. Explanation: The buyer bought the house in exchange for what? If in exchange for money (thaman), its detail has passed. And if in exchange for goods (urūḍ) other than gold and silver, then that goods have two forms: it is either from dhawaat al-qeem or from dhawaat al-amthal. In the first case, the preemptor will take the house by paying the value of that goods. In the second case, he will give its like. Because the Shariah has given the preemptor the right that he may become owner of the buyer's property without the buyer's consent. But he should receive exactly what the buyer gave in return. So as much as possible, full observance of giving the like should be made. Hence, if he is able to give the physical like (mithl shūrī), he gives the physical like; if not, then the equivalent in value. Physical like means like in form, i.e. in goods of like (dhawaat al-amthal); equivalent in value means price, i.e. in goods of value (dhawaat al-qeem). The same ruling applies if someone destroys another's goods. Then the author said that numerically close items (addad mutaqarib) are counted among dhawaat al-amthal. If someone sold his land and gave his own land in exchange, and there are preemptors for both lands, what will they pay to take the land? He said the preemptor of the seller's land will give the value of the buyer's land, and the preemptor of the buyer's land will give the value of the seller's land. Because land is among dhawaat al-qeem, so each preemptor takes it by paying its value. Note: 'Urd (goods) are valuable items that are not customary, like slaves etc. Dhawaat al-amthal: their like is found in the market with no difference; if there is difference but it is negligible customarily, it is still called mithliy. Dhawaat al-qeem: those whose like is not found in the markets. Adad mutaqarib: goods where there is no price difference between individual units.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter of shuf'ah (preemption) in Ashraf al-Hidayah. It addresses the case where the price (thaman) in the sale is not money but other goods, specifically land-for-land exchange or goods-for-property. The key distinction is between dhawaat al-qeem (goods of value, i.e., unique items whose like is not available in the market) and dhawaat al-amthal (fungible goods whose like can be obtained). The ruling: the preemptor must compensate the buyer with exactly what the buyer paid. If the price was a unique good, the preemptor pays its market value. If the price was a fungible good, he gives a like item. This is because the preemptor takes the property by legal right, which should not cause loss to the buyer. The author also explains 'addad mutaqarib (numerically close items) as a subcategory of dhawaat al-amthal—such items are considered fungible because price differences between individual units are negligible. The passage then applies this to land-for-land exchange: land is dhawaat al-qeem, so the preemptor of one land pays the value of the other land. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi position on shuf'ah.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to take over a sold property by paying the buyer the price.
ذوات القیم (dhawaat al-qeem): Goods whose like is not found in the market; compensation is by market value.
ذوات الامثال (dhawaat al-amthal): Fungible goods that can be replaced with a like item; compensation is by giving a similar item.
عددی متقارب (addad mutaqarib): Numerically close items where individual units have negligible price difference; treated as dhawaat al-amthal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a house is sold in exchange for a unique slave (dhawaat al-qeem), what must the preemptor give to take the house?
A) A similar slave (like)
B) The market value of the slave
C) The same slave
D) Nothing, because slaves cannot be used as price

Key Takeaway
The preemptor must compensate the buyer with exactly what the buyer gave: value for unique goods, like for fungible goods.
